    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2128813397" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2128813397" />
    <a title="Cris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/63364301-cris"><img alt="Cris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605625049p2/63364301.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2128813397?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cris" href="/user/show/63364301-cris">Cris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63364301-cris?shelf=netgalley">netgalley</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63364301-cris?shelf=read-in-english">read-in-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63364301-cris?shelf=stand-alone">stand-alone</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63364301-cris?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63364301-cris?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63364301-cris?shelf=3-stars">3-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/63364301-cris?shelf=read-in-2017">read-in-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2128813397" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8710132733236241514"><b>English/Italiano</b><br/><p>First of all, I have to say I am not a native english speaker so, please, I prefer to not be attacked, if I made any mistakes. Instead of do that, it would be nice if you corrected me, thank you :)</p><br/><p>
  <i>Most importantly, I would like to thank NetGalley and publishers for allowing me to read this book. Thank you very much.</i>
</p><br/><p><i>Actual rating</i>: <b>3.5 stars</b></p><br/><p>Writing this review won’t be easy because of <b>this book destroyed my heart and feelings</b>. Before starting reading <i>Dear Martin</i>, I automaticall</p></span>
  <span id="freeText8710132733236241514" style="display:none"><b>English/Italiano</b><br /><p>First of all, I have to say I am not a native english speaker so, please, I prefer to not be attacked, if I made any mistakes. Instead of do that, it would be nice if you corrected me, thank you :)</p><br /><p>
  <i>Most importantly, I would like to thank NetGalley and publishers for allowing me to read this book. Thank you very much.</i>
</p><br /><p><i>Actual rating</i>: <b>3.5 stars</b></p><br /><p>Writing this review won’t be easy because of <b>this book destroyed my heart and feelings</b>. Before starting reading <i>Dear Martin</i>, I automatically compared it to <i>The Hate U Give</i> by Angie Thomas, another YA contemporary book focused on #BlackLivesMatter movement. For the ones who don’t know what this movement is, it is based on racism towards black people and one of central themes is protesting against police, wherever a black person (especially unarmed teenagers) is killed by the ones who should make justice. The protests are placed mainly in The United States. Before reading both of these two novels, since I’m not American, I didn’t know so much about it. It isn’t okay, it’s important to know and fortunately I learnt everything I know from these two books.</p><br /><p>Let me tell a thing: <b>I’m glad authors such as Nic Stone and Angie Thomas exist</b>. I’m glad they published these books, both seriously important that <b>I recommend them to EVERYONE</b> (especially to white people!). Putting in the shoes of black people, black boy teenager in this book, made me understand a lot of their condition from when white people tend to judge them just for the colour of their skin. Of course I couldn’t feel it properly, but <b>this novel made me reflex about a lot of things and changed one of the prospective of my life</b>. Those are the books that I love the most: the ones who make me think and, because of that, never really leave me. For the people who are guessing if this book is like <i>The Hate U Give</i> (before reading I was guessing it too), I’d say to read them both because, although both of them are about #BlackLivesMatter, they are quite different.</p><br /><p>What does Dear Martin talk about? The story follows the life of a black seventeen-year-old boy named Justyce who just ended up arrested while he was helping his ex drunk girlfriend to get home safe, when a policeman saw him and, because of fact he is black, he was automatically considered as a ‘thug’. After that night, he starts writing letters to Martin Luther King Jr. and asking him for advice such as what happened that night and what happens, daily, at his school.</p><br /><p>Pretty much everyone knows who Martin Luther King Jr. was and how he fight racism to get black people rights in a non-violent way that inspires the main character. In the course of the all book he asks himself, and others too, <i>What Martin would do?</i> Or <i>Why be good if people see him as a criminal anyway?</i> I have to admit, those arguments were pretty philosophical.</p><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“So why even try to be ‘good’?” <br />“You can’t change how other people think and act, but you’re in full control of you. When it comes down to it, the only question that matters is this: If nothing in the world ever changes, what type of man are you gonna be?”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><p>In the book there are other aspects who are treated like friendship and romance. Unbelievably, I just found out a book where friendship between two boys is written great: Justyce has a best friend, Manny, he is black as well, and <b>I really enjoyed how the writer characterized their friendship</b>.</p><br /><p>I’d like to talk about my favourite character in the novel, Sarah-Jane. She is white, Justyce’s debate friend. Most of all, she truly supported race equality and always defended black people. For example, when Justyce got an higher score than a white guy to enter to Yale, he complains about how impossible that thing is. These are my favourite quotes of her:</p><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“Bottom line, nobody sees us [white people] and automatically assumes we’re up to no good.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“We’ll [white people] never be seen as criminals before we’re seen as people.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><p>Wow, see why I love her? <b>More people like her should exist</b>.</p><br /><p><u>Why I didn’t give it more stars:</u><br />There is <b>so much slang</b> in this book and I, since I’m not an English-speaker person, couldn’t understand some parts of it. Do really American people use slang as much as the characters of this book? I don’t think there should be so much in a book.</p><br /><p>Besides that, <b>I didn’t enjoy</b> how the writing sometimes was. To be more specific, there are <b>parts who seem like a script</b>. I didn’t appreciate that writing choice.</p><br /><p>Anyway, I hope to read more books by Nic Stone because <b>I think she’ll write other amazing books that will definitely be worthy</b>.</p><br /><br /><p>
  <b>Italiano</b>
  <br />
</p><p>
  <i>Soprattutto, vorrei ringraziare NetGalley e la casa editrice per avermi permesso di leggere questo libro. Grazie molte.</i>
</p><br /><p><i>In realtà</i>: <b>3.5 stelline</b></p><br /><p>Scrivere questa recensione non sarà facile perché <b>questo libro ha distrutto il mio cuore e i miei sentimenti</b>. Prima di iniziare a leggere <i>Dear Martin</i>, l’ho automaticamente paragonato a <i>The Hate U Give</i> di Angie Thomas (che è uscito in Italia lo scorso mese, mi sembra), un altro Young Adult contemporaneo basato sul movimento #BlackLivesMatter. Per coloro che non conoscessero questo movimento, è fondato sul razzismo contro le persone di colore e uno dei temi principali è protestare contro la polizia, ogni volta che una persona di colore (specialmente minorenni non armati) viene uccisa da chi dovrebbe fare giustizia. Le proteste sono maggiormente svolte negli Stati Uniti. Prima di leggere entrambi questi due romanzi, dato che non sono americana, non ne sapevo molto. Non va bene. È importante conoscere e per fortuna ho imparato tutto ciò che so da questi due libri.</p><br /><p>Lasciatemi dire una cosa: <b>sono contenta che esitano autrici come Nic Stone e Angie Thomas</b>. Sono contenta che abbiano pubblicato questi libri, entrambi davvero importanti, i quali <b>consiglio a TUTTI</b> (specialmente alle persone con la carnagione chiara. I bianchi, per intenderci!). Mettersi nei panni delle persone di colore, in questo libro di un ragazzo di colore, mi ha fatto capire molto della loro condizione a partire da quando i bianchi tendono a giudicarli solo per il colore della loro pelle. Ovviamente non ho posso capire completamente, ma <b>questo romanzo mi ha fatto riflettere su molte cose e cambiato una delle prospettive della mia vita</b>. Questi sono i libri che amo di più: quelli che mi fanno pensare e, per questo motivo, non mi lasciano mai. Per coloro che si stanno domandando se questo libro è come <i>The Hate U Give</i> (prima di leggerlo me lo chiedevo anche io), direi di leggerli entrambi perché, nonostante entrambi siano sul #BlackLivesMatter, sono abbastanza diversi.</p><br /><p>Di cosa parla Dear Martin? La storia segue la vita di un ragazzo di colore di diciassette anni chiamato Justyce che finisce in arresto mentre stava aiutando la sua ex ragazza ubriaca a tornare a casa sana e salva, quando un poliziotto lo vede e, a causa del fatto che sia di colore, viene automaticamente considerato come un ‘teppista’. Dopo quella notte, inizia a scrivere lettere a Martin Luther King Jr. e a chiedergli consigli per esempio su cosa è accaduto quella notte e cosa succede, giornalmente, alla sua scuola.</p><br /><p>Una buona maggioranza di persone sa chi è Martin Luther King Jr. e come ha combattuto contro il razzismo per ottenere i diritti per le persone di colore in un modo non violento che inspira il personaggio principale. Nel corso di tutto il libro si domanda, e anche ad altri, <i>Cosa farebbe Martin?</i> O <i>Perché essere buono se le persone lo vedono comunque come un criminale?</i> Deve ammetterlo, questi argomenti sono abbastanza filosofici.</p><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“Quindi perché addirittura provare a essere ‘buoni’?” <br />“Non puoi cambiare come le altre pensano o come si comportano, ma tu sei totalmente in controllo di te stesso. Quando si ritorna a questo, l’unica domanda che importa è questa: se niente nel mondo cambia, quale tipo di uomo hai intenzione di essere?”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><p>Nel libro ci sono altri aspetti che sono trattati come l’amicizia e la storia d’amore. Incredibilmente, ho appena scoperto un libro dove l’amicizia tra due ragazzi è scritta in modo fantastico: Justyce ha un migliore amico, Manny, anche lui di colore, e <b>mi è davvero piaciuto come la scrittrice abbia caratterizzato la loro amicizia </b>.</p><br /><p>Vorrei parlare del mio personaggio preferito nel romanzo, Sarah-Jane. È bianca, un’amica di dibattito di Justyce. Più di tutto, lei davvero supporta l’eguaglianza tra razze e ha sempre difeso le persone di colore. Per esempio, quando Justyce ha ottenuto un punteggio più alto di un ragazzo bianco per entrare a Yale, si lamenta (il ragazzo bianco) di quanto possa essere impossibile questa cosa. Ecco le mie citazioni preferite del suo personaggio:</p><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“Il punto è che, nessuno vede noi [persone bianche] e automaticamente pensa che non abbiamo buone intenzioni.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>“Noi [persone binache] non verremo mai viste come criminali prima di essere viste come persone.”</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><p>Wow, riuscite a capire perché la amo? <b>Più persone come lei dovrebbero esistere </b>.</p><br /><p><u>Perché non gli ho dato più stelline:</u><br />C’è <b>davvero molto slang</b> in questo libro e io, dato che non sono una persona che è nata in un paese dove si parla inglese, non ho potuto capire alcune parti di esso. Davvero gli americani usano così tanto slang come i personaggi di questo libro? Non credo ce ne dovrebbe essere così tanto in un libro.</p><br /><p>A parte quello, <b>non mi è piaciuto</b> come fosse lo stile di scrittura a volte. Per essere più specifica, ci sono <b>parti che sembrano come un copione </b>. Non ho apprezzato quella scelta di scrittura.</p><br /><p>Comunque, spero di leggere più libri scritti da Nic Stone perché <b>credo che scriverà altri meravigliosi libri che ne varranno decisamente la pena</b>.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="8710132733236241514" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1830766393?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1830766393" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9262425059943171921">Holy fucking shit I loved this book so much.<br/><br/>DEAR MARTIN is a masterpiece. There's simply no other way to describe it. It's a work of art that will leave you breathless and sobbing and unable to stop thinking about it. <br/><br/>I'm amazed by how well-done everything is: the characters, the story, the writing, the cover, the plot twists...It fully deserves 5 stars, and I wish I could give it more. <br/><br/>When this book releases, there's no excuse for not reading it. You simply have to. If you like contemporary? R</span>
  <span id="freeText9262425059943171921" style="display:none">Holy fucking shit I loved this book so much.<br /><br />DEAR MARTIN is a masterpiece. There's simply no other way to describe it. It's a work of art that will leave you breathless and sobbing and unable to stop thinking about it. <br /><br />I'm amazed by how well-done everything is: the characters, the story, the writing, the cover, the plot twists...It fully deserves 5 stars, and I wish I could give it more. <br /><br />When this book releases, there's no excuse for not reading it. You simply have to. If you like contemporary? Read it. If you don't like contemporary? Still read it. This is a book for EVERYONE, and it's a book that deserves every bit of the pre-publication praise it's gathering. <br /><br />Reread 9/10/17, because I couldn't help it. </span>

<div id="review_2054631974" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2054631974" />
    <a title="Malia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5953660-malia"><img alt="Malia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476920229p2/5953660.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2054631974?book_show_action=true">Jul 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Malia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Malia" href="/user/show/5953660-malia">Malia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5953660-malia?shelf=ya">ya</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2054631974" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6022821153464356970">A really moving, well-written and thought-provoking story, which delves into subjects such as race, violence, culture, identity and relationships. It is, at times, really difficult to read, because I felt confronted with a reality that I wish were different. For someone who has never been (at least to my knowledge) discriminated against because of the way I look, Dear Martin offered insight into the constant strain and struggle so many people face just in normal, day-to-day situations. The book </span>
  <span id="freeText6022821153464356970" style="display:none">A really moving, well-written and thought-provoking story, which delves into subjects such as race, violence, culture, identity and relationships. It is, at times, really difficult to read, because I felt confronted with a reality that I wish were different. For someone who has never been (at least to my knowledge) discriminated against because of the way I look, Dear Martin offered insight into the constant strain and struggle so many people face just in normal, day-to-day situations. The book was both disturbing and moving and though I thought Angie Thomas' <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/32075671.The_Hate_U_Give" title="The Hate U Give by Angie Thomas" rel="nofollow noopener">The Hate U Give</a> was very good, I think I liked this book, dealing with a similar situation, even better. It's the kind of story that should be read in school. Highly recommended!<br /><br />Find more reviews and bookish fun at <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.princessandpen.com">http://www.princessandpen.com</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="6022821153464356970" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2151540349" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2151540349" />
    <a title="J.T.  Yorke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72495241-j-t-yorke"><img alt="J.T.  Yorke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586312907p2/72495241.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2151540349?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J.T.  Yorke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J.T.  Yorke" href="/user/show/72495241-j-t-yorke">J.T.  Yorke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2151540349&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2151540349" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16833346275212644770">I read this book awhile ago but only reviewed it on my bookstagram. After a mini storm of rage from one black author over another black author's critique of this book, I felt compelled to return to it and leave my honest review. <br/><br/>The first time I read <i>Dear Martin</i>, I found it to be one of the weaker #BLM novels, a weak novel in general. But the shooting itself was so compelling and surprising, I gave the book a few bonus points. Then I sat down and really thought about the characterizarion of blac</span>
  <span id="freeText16833346275212644770" style="display:none">I read this book awhile ago but only reviewed it on my bookstagram. After a mini storm of rage from one black author over another black author's critique of this book, I felt compelled to return to it and leave my honest review. <br><br>The first time I read <i>Dear Martin</i>, I found it to be one of the weaker #BLM novels, a weak novel in general. But the shooting itself was so compelling and surprising, I gave the book a few bonus points. Then I sat down and really thought about the characterizarion of black women in the book. If you're not looking for it (and let's be honest, most readers don't care how black girls are repped in books since we're in so few of them), you might not notice it. But if you read this book <b>written by a black woman who attended Spelman with many other black women</b> you'll be appalled af at how she chooses to characterize black girls.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1524850105ra/25444738.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Plot:</b> Much like <i>Tyler Johnson is Here</i>, the inciting incident in <i>Dear Martin</i> doesn't come until halfway through the book. The beginning and middle of the story is just heavy handed lesson after lesson. No showing, all telling. The letters Justyce writes to Martin, I don't buy that a 17 year old would be doing something like that. In a middle grade book, definitely, but not YA. But he really doesn't try to live anything like MLK, it's just a diary in which he states repeatedly, "Martin, life is so hard now. I'm not supposed to like white women." <br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1471424933ra/20048095.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br><b>Characters:</b> Get ready, it's about to get messy. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1496471134ra/22916187.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Justyce:</b> This kid confused me. He comes from the hood and yet he acts like he has no idea about anything. Now the KKK scene, I believe this could happen, but <b>ONLY</b> to a black kid who desperately wants to be accepted as white. Justyce is too old and too aware of himself to have went along with that tomfoolery. Later he knocks Jared out for a lesser offense which, again, why did you not knock him out the first time? <br><br><b>Manny:</b> Manny makes the comment that black girls are angry and ghetto and he's afraid to go to Morehouse and meet the girls at Spelman. Sorry, but I don't buy for a second that his parents, as Pro-HBCU as they are, would have allowed their son to be completely whitewashed still at 17 years old. NOPE. How tf has he managed to never meet a black girl he wasn't related to? And because Manny is killed, we never get to see his comment rectified. He dies thinking black girls are all ghetto. <br><br><b>Sarah Jane:</b> White savior and a prize to be won. That's all she was. During every classroom debate about racism, it's always racist Jared VS "woke" Sarah Jane. There are plenty of woke white people, sure, but Sarah Jane's dialogue sounds like it came from a book on racism. She even tries to alienate herself from other white people by saying "my family didn't own slaves!" as though she's not currently reaping the benefits of slavery. <br><br>It's pretty clear she's meant to be the heroine of the story but as a character, she's simply a mouthpiece.<br><br><b>Melo Taylor:</b> Melo, Justyce's ex, is pretty much cast as an insatiable sex kitten. She's also biracial, having the facial features of a white woman but the body of a black woman, Justyce's dream come true. Even though he uses her for sex and status, Melo is completely 1D. She's nothing more than a sex object even though she attends the SAME SCHOOL as Justyce and Sarah Jane and therefore is probably just as intelligent if not more intelligent than they are. <br><br>Idk how it is that she's only ever seen switching her hips through the hall and getting jealous of Sarah Jane. <br><br><b>Justyce's Mom:</b> Although Justyce is raised by a single parent and wouldn't be in this ritzy ass school if it wasn't for his mother, he dogs his mom tf out. She's painted as as shrew who doesn't understand or care what he's going through as a young black man (Sarah Jane does, but nope, the grown black woman who birthed him doesn't give af). <br><br>Literally all this woman does is tell Justyce <b>"DON'T BRING THAT WHITE GIRL HOME!"</b> Are there black parents who feel this way? Sure. But to the point where they address it at the <i>funeral</i> of their son's best friend? Give me a fucking break. &amp; parents who are that invested in what race their kids date would generally be more invested in their other problems as well. Why is mom starkly absent when Justyce gets manhandled by cops but whenever talk of Sarah Jane occurs, suddenly she's popping up out of manholes in the street like a Ninja Turtle to cockblock? <b>NO</b>.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1524851675ra/25444917.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>&amp; to make her look even worse, Sarah's parents supposedly love Justyce like he's their own son. Rich white people in predominantly white area (do they have these in Atlanta?) have no prejudice against young black boy from the hood who almost joined a gang and witnessed his best friend murdered AND whose mom hates white people and won't even shake their hand? <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1524851675ra/25444918.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Sorry, but this whole book reeks of <b>WHITE IS RIGHT</b>. The white parents are better, the white love interest is better, nearly all of the black characters in this story are trying to ruin Justyce's life, including his own mom. <br><br>Now, this isn't to say you can't have positive white people in a book about #BLM. Of course you can! But how great are these particular white characters if they're being contrasted against the worst stereotypes of black people (women in particular)? <br><br>I mean, is Sarah Jane still appealing if we don't have Justyce's mom talking shit about white girls in the peanut gallery? Or the black sex kitten Melo Taylor in a drunken stupor as Justyce gets his ass beat by a cop because of her?<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1466696048ra/19509450.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Writing:</b> Lazy as hell. Stone sold this book before actually writing it and it shows. The dialogue is written like a script. There's no clear point to it except some writers can't do dialogue very well. I can tell you as a writer that it's much easier to write dialogue as a script and not worry about identifying the speaker and what they're doing while they're talking. <br><br>But it <i>does not</i> make for a better read. All the other bs with the story set aside, I don't like her writing style at all. It's not good. There's no setting, you don't know where these characters are half the time or what anything around them looks like. It's half-assed.<br><br><b>Do I recommend?</b> <br><br> <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1524850105ra/25444740.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>If the roles were reversed, a YA book in which white girls were only cast as 1D sex kittens and racists, would have never made it to publication. &amp; if by chance it ever did, it would NOT be receiving rave reviews. <br><br>But black girls are double minorities. The world of publishing doesn't really give af about us, as evidenced by the amount of black girls you see on book covers today. <br><br>Now, if I was white, I would have read the story differently. I would have championed Sarah Jane because she's someone like me. But you have to ask yourself, if you were a black girl, who do you champion in this story? There is no positive representation for them and it's very strange to consider the book was written by a black woman.<br><br>Some people tend to believe that minorities aren't capable of being problematic. Trust me, we are. There are black people who vehemently oppose the Black Lives Matter movement. There are black people who approve the use of the N-word. There are black women who don't really like other black women. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1524851675ra/25444919.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>While it may be difficult for authors of color to get published, they don't get a pass to write without critique. Some writers gain a massive following by outing racist books but then turn around and try to build a force field around books by black authors. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1504342981ra/23747358.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Nah. I love reading and writing and holding authors of ALL COLORS accountable for the work they put out. I don't care if it's my blood sister, if the book is problematic, it will be dragged to filth, as it should be. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1490369200ra/22306864.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="16833346275212644770" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2359925845" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2359925845" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2359925845?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=ya-fall-2018">ya-fall-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=ya-adult">ya-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=ya-race">ya-race</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=ya-afram">ya-afram</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2359925845" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12237185645861277121">“You can’t change how other people think and act, but you’re in full control of you. When it comes down to it, the only question that matters is this: If nothing in the world ever changes, what type of man are you gonna be?” <br/><br/>Dear Martin is one of several YA novels that seem to have emerged in response to the killing of young black people by police officers in the U. S. and in keeping with the Black Lives Matter movement. Since it is YA and is likely going to be adopted in schools, it can provide</span>
  <span id="freeText12237185645861277121" style="display:none">“You can’t change how other people think and act, but you’re in full control of you. When it comes down to it, the only question that matters is this: If nothing in the world ever changes, what type of man are you gonna be?” <br /><br />Dear Martin is one of several YA novels that seem to have emerged in response to the killing of young black people by police officers in the U. S. and in keeping with the Black Lives Matter movement. Since it is YA and is likely going to be adopted in schools, it can provide an opportunity for discussions that can link to current events. Others in this conversation include The Hate U Give by Angie Thomas and Jason Reynolds and Brendan Kiely’s All American Boys, both of which I read and liked. As of this writing, I will also read Ibi Zoboi’s American Street soon. And Kwame Alexander’s Booked. I write this during the Laquan McDonald trial here in Chicago in fall 2018, so I timed my reading of this now with my YA class (which is focused mainly on romance and gothic horror, though there’s both romance and "horror" in Dear Martin, too).<br /><br />What I look for in a YA novel about race is not much different than what I look for in any YA novel, or any novel: For it to be a novel, and not didactic. To explore issues, and not preach about them. This one opens pretty grippingly and not surprisingly with Justyce, a black teen, arrested as (white) cops see him wrestling his (white-appearing) drunk girlfriend Melo into the back seat of a car. Justyce (like real life Angie Thomas) lived in the city, but now boards at an exclusive prep school with mostly white kids, and is (he finds later) going to Yale; his debate partner, SJ, is white, Jewish, going to Columbia U. Jus’s best friend is Manny, a black guy who has some (white) friends who question affirmative action and so on. Manny is also (white) Jared's best friend.<br /><br />When we begin the novel Jus is already beginning a project for himself, a series of letters to Dr. Martin Luther King to help him reflect on his (own) life. The arrest pushes Jus a little faster down a road of racial awareness, and he talks to his mama around then about MLK and about how it is important for us to live together. Jus listens to a class discussion on equality, and then participates in an ill-advised Halloween parade dressed us a “ghetto thug” as a white kid goes full-on KKK, hood and all. Bad decision.<br /><br />The road to racial awareness gets rockier when Jus, playing music too loud in a a car, is profaned by the N-word, shot by a cop, and a (key person, I’m not sayin’) is also shot and killed. Jus considers joining the Black Jihad gang, as a result. This process of considering gang membership, and meeting with the leader of said gang, like the ill-advised Halloween dress-up, comes back to bite Jus. I don’t always believe everything that happens in this book, but I like a couple surprises that happen in it, and I like the way it moves to the end, with a complicated trial, continued connections with (anti-affirmative-action) Jared, SJ, and MLK.<br /><br />“You ever consider that maybe you not supposed to 'fit'? People who make history rarely do.”<br /><br />I like:<br /><br />*the fact that Jus adopts a project, to write letters to Martin Luther King, as a way of exploring how to live his life.<br />*that (white) Jewish SJ is the most articulate spokesperson for racial justice issues in two classroom discussions (though I can imagine some people might not like this). The fact that some white people can be allies in the fight is important in this book.<br />*I like the classroom discussions on equality in Doc’s class, though it is clear they are all prep school articulate; they don’t sound like “average” kids.<br />*Teacher Doc, a black guy and Jus’s mentor, who also went to an exclusive white prep school and experienced racism.<br />*Stone’s point that class and education do not protect you from prejudice. Coates in his memoir tells a real life story about a black friend who was rich and well educated but killed, nevertheless.<br />*I like it that activist mama is just not ready yet about his son dating white girls (she’s complicated, feels real).<br />*I like that last scene at the graveyard. Hey, people can change!<br /><br />Spoiler issues I have trouble with in the book:<br /><br />*Melo is dismissed as a “ho” by some in the book, and she never gets fully respected.<br />*I don’t quite buy that smart future Yalie, after taking with his mom about MLK, would actually get dressed up as a “thug” for Halloween with other stereotypically- dressed kids (one goes full on KKK and pays for that). I might possibly buy he considers joining a gang, but I don’t quite believe it.<br />*Not enough Jus introspection spent on the grieving of the kid who is killed (mentioned above).<br />*I wish Jus would have more fully engaged with the ideas of MLK; like: quote some, mention actual events? I know Jus is young and newly engaged in justice issues, but the actual MLK conversation seems flat and generalized and shallow. I guess the very fact that he dresses as a “thug” and considers joining a violent gang illustrates he doesn’t fully understand the idea of MLK (yet).<br />*The Yale roommate is stereotypically dressed white prep; hmm, can we consider he may not be racist? I suppose Jared’s example can be considered here.<br />*SJ and Jus binge watch National Geographic, which could be funny, but maybe we could learned of their interest in science.<br /><br />If you were teaching this book, I’d read it with Ta Nehisi Coates’s Between the World and Me and James Baldwin’s The Fire Next Time and lots of stuff all too easy to find on the internet today. <br /><br />I think this is a good book for discussion and my students like it. I don't think it is too preachy, and in the SJ-Jus connection, and with the ending, is complex about racial relations in places. I think it’s pretty engaging.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12237185645861277121" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer6057780807320273232">I read this book for the Goodreads' book club Diversity in All Forms! If you would like to participate in the discussion here is the link: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/19118918-dear-martin-february-2018'>https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/...</a><br/><br/>I really enjoyed this book! It was short, but covered so many important topics. The main character is Justyce McAllister. He is a African-American male, high school senior that is at the top of his class. The book starts off with a police officer putting him in handcuffs because he was helping his ex-girlfriend late at </span>
  <span id="freeText6057780807320273232" style="display:none">I read this book for the Goodreads' book club Diversity in All Forms! If you would like to participate in the discussion here is the link: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/19118918-dear-martin-february-2018">https://www.goodreads.com/topic/show/...</a><br /><br />I really enjoyed this book! It was short, but covered so many important topics. The main character is Justyce McAllister. He is a African-American male, high school senior that is at the top of his class. The book starts off with a police officer putting him in handcuffs because he was helping his ex-girlfriend late at night (she looks white), because she is drunk. Throughout the whole book Justyce speaks out and notices all the injustice that is happening to him and around him. He writes letters to Dr. Martin Luther King Jr. for answers on what to do.</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JHbOpFX6S2P1E9PCvPgxUcbC1o32UzNjcn6PiofuYB0J4sZqMthrArJ/ZyxkVR+Kvy4qMIuRxDO9gMa7xJgvaQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('z8Jx8NvEyuInyxax3WuPjofdwyvdSutTkyvVZCWO6R3iVnk+vObqg2Cnol8FxqFV/jE/lqCIHANc1ZxVZvimaQ==')})
  }

This remarkable reimagining of Dr. Ibram X. Kendi\'s National Book Award-winning Stamped from the Beginning reveals the history of racist ideas in America, and inspires hope for an antiracist future. It takes you on a race journey from then to now, shows you why we feel how we feel, and why the poison of racism lingers. It also proves that while racist ideas have always been easy to fabricate and distribute, they can also be discredited. Through a gripping, fast-paced, and energizing narrative written by beloved award-winner Jason Reynolds, this book shines a light on the many insidious forms of racist ideas--and on ways readers can <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer423743439822422154">Nic Stone was born and raised in a suburb of Atlanta, GA, and the only thing she loves more than an adventure is a good story about one. After graduating from Spelman College, she worked extensively in teen mentoring and lived in Israel for a few years before returning to the US to write full-time. Growing up with a wide range of cultures, religions, and backgrounds, Stone strives to bring these d</span>
  <span id="freeText423743439822422154" style="display:none">Nic Stone was born and raised in a suburb of Atlanta, GA, and the only thing she loves more than an adventure is a good story about one. After graduating from Spelman College, she worked extensively in teen mentoring and lived in Israel for a few years before returning to the US to write full-time. Growing up with a wide range of cultures, religions, and backgrounds, Stone strives to bring these diverse voices and stories to her work.<br /><br />Stone lives in Atlanta with her husband and two sons. You can find her on Twitter and Instagram at @getnicced or on her website nicstone.info.<br /></span>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/44640226-dear-martin">Quotes from Dear Martin</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;You can’t change how other people think and act, but you’re in full control of you. When it comes down to it, the only question that matters is this: If nothing in the world ever changes, what type of man are you gonna be?&rdquo;</span>
